   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Passover
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>

     <div class="mw-parser-output">

       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Passover_(disambiguation)" title="Passover (disambiguation)">
        Passover (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>

        </th>

         <b>

         –
         <b>
          פסח
         </b>
         (in
         <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
          Hebrew
         </a>
         ).
        </td>

        <th scope="row">
         Observed by
        </th>

         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
           Jews
          </a>
         </b>
         . (In various forms also by:
         <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">
          Samaritans
         </a>
         ;
         <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">
          Messianic Jews
         </a>
         ;
         <a href="/wiki/Passover_(Christian_holiday)" title="Passover (Christian holiday)">
          Christians
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Groups_claiming_affiliation_with_Israelites" title="Groups claiming affiliation with Israelites">
          some groups claiming affiliation with Israelites
         </a>
         ).
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Type
        </th>
        <td>
         Jewish and Samaritan (One of the
         <a href="/wiki/Three_Pilgrimage_Festivals" title="Three Pilgrimage Festivals">
          Three Pilgrimage Festivals
         </a>
         ), cultural
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Significance
        </th>
        <td class="summary">
         Celebrates
         <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
          The Exodus
         </a>
         , the freedom from
         <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
          slavery
         </a>
         of the
         <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
          Children of Israel
         </a>
         from
         <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Egypt" title="History of ancient Egypt">
          ancient Egypt
         </a>
         that followed the
         <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt" title="Plagues of Egypt">
          Ten Plagues
         </a>
         .
         <br/>
         Beginning of the 49 days of
         <a href="/wiki/Counting_of_the_Omer" title="Counting of the Omer">
          Counting of the Omer
         </a>
         <br/>
         Connected to barley harvest in spring.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Celebrations
        </th>
        <td>
         In Jewish practice, one or two festive
         <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">
          Seder
         </a>
         meals – first two nights; in the times of the
         <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">
          Temple in Jerusalem
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Passover_sacrifice" title="Passover sacrifice">
          Passover sacrifice
         </a>
         . In Samaritan practice, men gather for a religious ceremony on
         <a href="/wiki/Mount_Gerizim" title="Mount Gerizim">
          Mount Gerizim
         </a>
         that includes the ancient lamb sacrifice (7th day)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Begins
        </th>
        <td>
         15th day of
         <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">
          Nisan
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-1">
           [1]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-2">
           [2]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Ends
        </th>

         21st day of Nisan in
         <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
          Israel
         </a>
         , and among some liberal
         <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">
          Diaspora Jews
         </a>
         ; 22nd day of Nisan outside Israel among more traditional Diaspora Jews.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
          <a href="#cite_note-3">
           [3]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Passover
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        Pesach
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
            p
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="/x/: 'ch' in 'loch'">
            x
           </span>
          </span>
          ,
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
            p
           </span>
           <span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">
            eɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">
            ɑː
           </span>
           <span title="/x/: 'ch' in 'loch'">
            x
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        פֶּסַח
       </span>
       ‬
       <i>
        Pesah, Pesakh
       </i>
       ) is a major, biblically derived
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_holidays" title="Jewish holidays">
        Jewish holiday
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
        Jews
       </a>
       celebrate Passover as a commemoration of their liberation by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Names_of_god_in_Judaism" title="Names of god in Judaism">
        God
       </a>
       from slavery in
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        ancient Egypt
       </a>
       and their freedom as a nation under the leadership of
       <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
        Moses
       </a>
       . It commemorates the story of
       <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
        the Exodus
       </a>
       as described in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
        Hebrew Bible
       </a>
       , especially in the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">
        Book of Exodus
       </a>
       , in which the
       <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
        Israelites
       </a>
       were freed from slavery in Egypt. According to standard
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_chronology" title="Biblical chronology">
        biblical chronology
       </a>
       , this event would have taken place at about 1300 BCE (
       <a href="/wiki/Anno_Mundi" title="Anno Mundi">
        AM
       </a>
       2450).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Passover is a spring festival which during the existence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">
        Temple in Jerusalem
       </a>
       was connected to the offering of the "first-fruits of the barley", barley being the first grain to ripen and to be harvested in the
       <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">
        Land of Israel
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barley_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Barley-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Passover commences on the 15th of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">
        Hebrew month
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">
        Nisan
       </a>
       and lasts for either seven days (in
       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       and for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reform_Jews" title="Reform Jews">
        Reform Jews
       </a>
       and other progressive Jews around the world who adhere to the Biblical commandment) or eight days for
       <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">
        Orthodox
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">
        Hasidic
       </a>
       , and most
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">
        Conservative
       </a>
       Jews (in the
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">
        diaspora
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
        Judaism
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Day#Boundaries_of_the_day" title="Day">
        day
       </a>
       commences at
       <a href="/wiki/Dusk" title="Dusk">
        dusk
       </a>
       and lasts until the following dusk, thus the first day of Passover begins after dusk of the 14th of Nisan and ends at dusk of the 15th day of the month of Nisan. The rituals unique to the Passover celebrations commence with the
       <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">
        Passover Seder
       </a>
       when the 15th of Nisan has begun. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">
        Northern Hemisphere
       </a>
       Passover takes place in
       <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">
        spring
       </a>
       as the
       <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
        Torah
       </a>
       prescribes it: "in the month of [the] spring" (בחדש האביב
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0223.htm#15" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 23:15
       </a>
       ). It is one of the most widely celebrated
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_holidays" title="Jewish holidays">
        Jewish holidays
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the narrative of the Exodus, the Bible tells that God helped the
       <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
        Children of Israel
       </a>
       escape from their slavery in Egypt by inflicting
       <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt" title="Plagues of Egypt">
        ten plagues
       </a>
       upon the ancient Egyptians before the Pharaoh would release his Israelite slaves; the tenth and worst of the plagues was the
       <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt#10._Death_of_the_firstborn_(מַכַּת_בְּכוֹרוֹת):_Ex._11:1–12:36" title="Plagues of Egypt">
        death of the Egyptian first-born
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Israelites were instructed to mark the doorposts of their homes with the blood of a slaughtered spring lamb and, upon seeing this, the spirit of the Lord knew to
       <i>
        pass over
       </i>
       the first-born in these homes, hence the English name of the holiday.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When the Pharaoh freed the Israelites, it is said that they left in such a hurry that they could not wait for bread dough to rise (leaven). In commemoration, for the duration of Passover no
       <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">
        leavened bread
       </a>
       is eaten, for which reason Passover was called the feast of unleavened bread in the Torah or Old Testament.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
         matzo
        </a>
       </i>
       (flat unleavened bread) is eaten during Passover and it is a tradition of the holiday.
      </p>
      <p>
       Historically, together with
       <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">
        Shavuot
       </a>
       ("Pentecost") and
       <a href="/wiki/Sukkot" title="Sukkot">
        Sukkot
       </a>
       ("Tabernacles"), Passover is one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Three_Pilgrimage_Festivals" title="Three Pilgrimage Festivals">
        Three Pilgrimage Festivals
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Shalosh Regalim
       </i>
       ) during which the entire population of the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
        kingdom of Judah
       </a>
       made a pilgrimage to the Temple in Jerusalem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">
        Samaritans
       </a>
       still make this pilgrimage to
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Gerizim" title="Mount Gerizim">
        Mount Gerizim
       </a>
       , but only men participate in public worship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_duration">
        Date and duration
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">
        Hebrew calendar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Passover begins on the 15th day of the month of
       <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">
        Nisan
       </a>
       , which typically falls in March or April of the
       <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">
        Gregorian calendar
       </a>
       . Passover is a spring festival, so the 15th day of Nisan typically begins on the night of a
       <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">
        full moon
       </a>
       after the northern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernal_equinox" title="Vernal equinox">
        vernal equinox
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, due to
       <a href="/wiki/Intercalation_(timekeeping)" title="Intercalation (timekeeping)">
        intercalary months
       </a>
       or leap months falling after the vernal equinox, Passover sometimes starts on the second full moon after vernal equinox, as in 2016.
      </p>
      <p>
       To ensure that Passover did not start before spring, the tradition in ancient Israel held that the first day of Nisan would not start until the barley was ripe, being the test for the onset of spring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If the barley was not ripe, or various other phenomena
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       indicated that spring was not yet imminent, an intercalary month (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adar_II" title="Adar II">
        Adar II
       </a>
       ) would be added. However, since at least the 4th century, the date has been fixed mathematically.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       , Passover is the seven-day holiday of the Feast of Unleavened Bread, with the first and last days celebrated as legal holidays and as holy days involving holiday meals, special prayer services, and abstention from work; the intervening days are known as
       <a href="/wiki/Chol_HaMoed" title="Chol HaMoed">
        Chol HaMoed
       </a>
       ("Weekdays [of] the Festival"). Diaspora Jews historically celebrated the festival for eight days.
       <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">
        Reform
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Reconstructionist_Judaism" title="Reconstructionist Judaism">
        Reconstructionist
       </a>
       Jews and Israeli Jews, wherever they are, usually celebrate the holiday over seven days. The reason for this extra day is due to enactment of the ancient Jewish sages.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       It is thought by many scholars that Jews outside of Israel could not be certain if their local calendars fully conformed to practice of the Temple at Jerusalem, so they added an extra day. But as this practice attaches only to certain (major) sacred days, others posit the extra day may have been added to accommodate people who had to travel long distances to participate in communal worship and ritual practices; or the practice may have evolved as a compromise between conflicting interpretations of Jewish Law regarding the calendar; or it may have evolved as a safety measure in areas where Jews were commonly in danger, so that their enemies would not be certain on which day to attack.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Karaite_Judaism" title="Karaite Judaism">
        Karaites
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">
        Samaritans
       </a>
       use different versions of the Jewish calendar, which are often out of sync with the modern Jewish calendar by one or two days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <i>
        2009
       </i>
       , for example, Nisan 15 on the Jewish calendar used by
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">
        Rabbinic Judaism
       </a>
       corresponds to April 9. On the calendars used by Karaites and Samaritans,
       <i>
        Abib
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Aviv
       </i>
       15 (as opposed to 'Nisan') corresponds to April 11 in
       <i>
        2009
       </i>
       . The Karaite and Samaritan Passovers are each one day long, followed by the six-day Festival of Unleavened Bread – for a total of seven days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_Biblical_development">
        Origins and Biblical development
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="888" data-file-width="776" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg/220px-Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg/330px-Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg/440px-Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Israel%27s_Escape_from_Egypt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Illustration of
         <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
          The Exodus
         </a>
         from Egypt, 1907
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The origins of the Passover festival antedate
       <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
        the Exodus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Passover ritual, prior to Deuteronomy, is widely thought to have its origins in an
       <a href="/wiki/Apotropaic_magic" title="Apotropaic magic">
        apotropaic
       </a>
       rite, unrelated to the Exodus, to ensure the protection of a family home, a rite conducted wholly within a clan.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyssop" title="Hyssop">
        Hyssop
       </a>
       was employed to daub the blood of a slaughtered sheep on the lintels and door posts to ensure that demonic forces could not enter the home.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A further hypothesis maintains that, once the
       <a href="/wiki/Priestly_Code" title="Priestly Code">
        Priestly Code
       </a>
       was promulgated, the exodus narrative took on a central function, as the apotropaic rite was, arguably, amalgamated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
        Canaanite
       </a>
       agricultural festival of spring which was a ceremony of Unleavened Bread, connected with the barley harvest. As the Exodus motif grew, the original function and symbolism of these double origins was lost.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Several motifs replicate the features associated with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" title="Mesopotamian">
        Mesopotamian
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Akitu" title="Akitu">
        Akitu
       </a>
       festival.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other scholars,
       <a href="/wiki/John_Van_Seters" title="John Van Seters">
        John Van Seters
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Judah_Segal" title="Judah Segal">
        J.B.Segal
       </a>
       and Tamara Prosic disagree with the merged two-festivals hypothesis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Called the "festival [of] the
       <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
        matzot
       </a>
       " (Hebrew: חג המצות
       <i>
        hag hamatzot
       </i>
       ) in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
        Hebrew Bible
       </a>
       , the commandment to keep Passover is recorded in the
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Leviticus" title="Book of Leviticus">
        Book of Leviticus
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        In the first month, on the fourteenth day of the month at dusk is the LORD's Passover. And on the fifteenth day of the same month is the feast of unleavened bread unto the LORD; seven days ye shall eat unleavened bread. In the first day ye shall have a holy convocation; ye shall do no manner of servile work. And ye shall bring an offering made by fire unto the LORD seven days; in the seventh day is a holy convocation; ye shall do no manner of servile work. (
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0323.htm#5" rel="nofollow">
         Leviticus 23:5–8
        </a>
        )
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The biblical regulations for the observance of the festival require that all leavening be disposed of before the beginning of the 15th of Nisan
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An unblemished lamb or goat, known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korban_Pesach" title="Korban Pesach">
        Korban Pesach
       </a>
       or "Paschal Lamb", is to be set apart on Nisan 10,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and slaughtered at dusk as Nisan 14 ends in preparation for the 15th of Nisan when it will be eaten after being roasted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The literal meaning of the Hebrew is "between the two evenings".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is then to be eaten "that night", Nisan 15,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exodus_12:8_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Exodus_12:8-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       roasted, without the removal of its internal organs
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with unleavened bread, known as
       <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
        matzo
       </a>
       , and bitter herbs known as
       <a href="/wiki/Maror" title="Maror">
        maror
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exodus_12:8_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Exodus_12:8-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nothing of the sacrifice on which the sun rises by the morning of the 15th of Nisan may be eaten, but must be burned.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The sacrifices may be performed only in a specific place prescribed by God (for Judaism, Jerusalem, and for Samaritans, Mount Gerizim).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The biblical regulations pertaining to the original Passover, at the time of the Exodus only, also include how the meal was to be eaten: "with your loins girded, your shoes on your feet, and your staff in your hand; and ye shall eat it in haste: it is the
       <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">
        LORD
       </a>
       's passover"
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#11" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:11
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The biblical requirements of slaying the Paschal lamb in the individual homes of the Hebrews and smearing the blood of the lamb on their doorways were celebrated in Egypt. However, once Israel was in the wilderness and the tabernacle was in operation, a change was made in those two original requirements (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0516.htm#2" rel="nofollow">
        Deuteronomy 16:2–6
       </a>
       ). Passover lambs were to be sacrificed at the door of the tabernacle and no longer in the homes of the Jews. No longer, therefore, could blood be smeared on doorways.
      </p>
      <p>
       The biblical commandments concerning the Passover (and the Feast of Unleavened Bread) stress the importance of remembering:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        And thou shalt remember that thou wast a bondman in Egypt; and thou shalt observe and do these statutes" (
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0516.htm#12" rel="nofollow">
         Deuteronomy 16:12
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#14" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 12:14
        </a>
        commands, in reference to God's sparing of the firstborn from the
        <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt#10._Death_of_the_firstborn_(מַכַּת_בְּכוֹרוֹת):_Ex._11:1–12:36" title="Plagues of Egypt">
         Tenth Plague
        </a>
        : And this day shall be unto you for a memorial, and ye shall keep it a feast to the LORD; throughout your generations ye shall keep it a feast by an ordinance for ever.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0213.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 13:3
        </a>
        repeats the command to remember: Remember this day, in which you came out of Egypt, out of the house of bondage, for by strength the hand of the LORD brought you out from this place.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_extra-biblical_sources">
        In extra-biblical sources
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some of these details can be corroborated, and to some extent amplified, in extrabiblical sources. The removal (or "sealing up") of the leaven is referred to in the
       <a href="/wiki/Elephantine_papyri" title="Elephantine papyri">
        Elephantine papyri
       </a>
       , an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       papyrus from 5th century BCE Elephantine in Egypt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The slaughter of the lambs on the 14th is mentioned in
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jubilees" title="Jubilees">
         The Book of Jubilees
        </a>
       </i>
       , a Jewish work of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_period" title="Ptolemaic period">
        Ptolemaic period
       </a>
       , and by the
       <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">
        Herodian
       </a>
       -era writers
       <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">
        Josephus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Philo" title="Philo">
        Philo
       </a>
       . These sources also indicate that "between the two evenings" was taken to mean the afternoon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Jubilees
       </i>
       states the sacrifice was eaten that night,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and together with Josephus states that nothing of the sacrifice was allowed to remain until morning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Philo states that the banquet included hymns and prayers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

       <tr>
        <td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:none;padding-top:none;line-height:1.7em;">

          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Category:Jews_and_Judaism" title="Category:Jews and Judaism">
            Category: Jews and Judaism
           </a>
          </li>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Portal:Judaism" title="Portal:Judaism">
            Portal: Judaism
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Jews_and_Judaism_sidebar" title="Template:Jews and Judaism sidebar">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Jews_and_Judaism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Jews and Judaism sidebar">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Jews_and_Judaism_sidebar&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The English term "Passover" is first known to be recorded in the
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English language
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">
        William Tyndale
       </a>
       's translation of the Bible, later appearing in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_James_Version_of_the_Bible" title="King James Version of the Bible">
        King James Version
       </a>
       as well. It is a literal translation of the Hebrew term.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', 'Keter Aram Tsova', 'Taamey Ashkenaz', 'Taamey David CLM', 'Taamey Frank CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Keter YG', 'Shofar', 'David CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', 'Simple CLM', 'Nachlieli', 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', Cardo, Alef, 'Noto Serif Hebrew', 'Noto Sans Hebrew', 'David Libre', David, 'Times New Roman', Gisha, Arial, FreeSerif, FreeSans;">
        פֶּסַח
       </span>
       ‬ is rendered as
       <a href="/wiki/Tiberian_Hebrew" title="Tiberian Hebrew">
        Tiberian
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">
         [pɛsaħ]
        </a>
       </span>
       <small class="nowrap">
        (
        <span class="unicode haudio">
         <span class="fn">
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/File:He-Pesach.ogg" title="About this sound">
            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/He-Pesach.ogg" title="He-Pesach.ogg">
           listen
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </small>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">
        Modern Hebrew
       </a>
       :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">
         [ˈpesaχ]
        </a>
       </span>
       <i>
        Pesah, Pesakh;
       </i>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       word is Latinized variously as
       <i>
        Peysekh, Paysakh, Paysokh
       </i>
       . The etymology is disputed, and hypotheses are divided whether to connect it to
       <i>
        psh
       </i>
       (to protect, save) or to a word meaning 'limp, dance with limping motions.' Cognate languages yield similar terms with distinct meanings, such as 'make soft, soothe, placate' (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
        Akkadian
        <i>
         passahu
        </i>
       </a>
       ), 'harvest, commemoration, blow' (
       <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">
        Egyptian
       </a>
       ), or 'separate' (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
        <i>
         fsh
        </i>
       </a>
       <i>
        )
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The verb "
       <i>
        pasàch
       </i>
       " (
       <span lang="he" title="Hebrew language text" xml:lang="he">
        פָּסַח
       </span>
       ) is first mentioned in the
       <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
        Torah
       </a>
       's account of
       <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">
        the Exodus
       </a>
       from Egypt (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#23" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:23
       </a>
       ), and there is some debate about its exact meaning: the commonly held assumption that it means "He passed over" (
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%A4%D7%A1%D7%97" title="wikt:פסח">
        פסח
       </a>
       ), in reference to God "passing over" (or "skipping") the houses of the Hebrews during the final of the Ten
       <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt" title="Plagues of Egypt">
        Plagues of Egypt
       </a>
       , stems from the translation provided in the
       <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">
        Septuagint
       </a>
       (παρελευσεται in
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#23" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:23
       </a>
       , and εσκεπασεν in
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#27" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:27
       </a>
       ).
       <a href="/wiki/Targum_Onkelos" title="Targum Onkelos">
        Targum Onkelos
       </a>
       translates
       <i>
        pesach
       </i>
       as וְיֵחוֹס "he had pity" coming from the Hebrew root ‘
       <b>
        חסה
       </b>
       ’ meaning to have pity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Judging from other instances of the verb, and instances of
       <a href="/wiki/Parallelism_(rhetoric)" title="Parallelism (rhetoric)">
        parallelism
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2013)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       a more faithful translation may be "he hovered over, guarding." Indeed, this is the image invoked by the verb in
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">
        Isaiah
       </a>
       31:5: "As birds hovering, so will the Lord of hosts protect Jerusalem; He will deliver it as He protecteth it, He will rescue it as He
       <i>
        passeth over
       </i>
       " (כְּצִפֳּרִים עָפוֹת – כֵּן יָגֵן יְהוָה צְבָאוֹת, עַל-יְרוּשָׁלִָם; גָּנוֹן וְהִצִּיל, פָּסֹחַ וְהִמְלִיט.) (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt1031.htm#5" rel="nofollow">
        Isaiah 31:5
       </a>
       ) Both meanings become apparent in Exodus 12:23 when parsed as: the Lord will pass (hover, guard) over the door, and will not suffer the destroyer (destroying angel is commanded to pass by the children of Israel) to come in unto your houses to smite.
      </p>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        Pesach
       </i>
       (Hebrew:
       <span lang="he" title="Hebrew language text" xml:lang="he">
        פֶּסַח
       </span>
       ) may also refer to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" title="Domestic sheep">
        lamb
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_goat" title="Domestic goat">
        goat
       </a>
       which was designated as the Passover sacrifice (called the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korban_Pesach" title="Korban Pesach">
         Korban Pesach
        </a>
       </i>
       in Hebrew). Four days before the Exodus, the Hebrews were commanded to set aside a lamb (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:3
       </a>
       ), and inspect it daily for blemishes. During the day on the 14th of Nisan, they were to slaughter the animal and use its blood to mark their
       <a href="/wiki/Post_and_lintel" title="Post and lintel">
        lintels and door posts
       </a>
       . Up until midnight on the 15th of Nisan, they were to consume the lamb. On the night of the first Passover at the start of the original Exodus, each family (or group of families) gathered together to eat a meal that included the meat of the
       <i>
        Korban Pesach
       </i>
       while the Tenth Plague ravaged Egypt.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Passover_offering.2C_Korban_Pesach">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Passover_offering,_Korban_Pesach">
        Passover offering,
        <i>
         Korban Pesach
        </i>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korban_Pesach" title="Korban Pesach">
        Korban Pesach
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The main entity in Passover according to Judaism is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korban_Pesach" title="Korban Pesach">
        sacrificial lamb
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bokser_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bokser-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the existence of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabernacle_(Judaism)" title="Tabernacle (Judaism)">
        Tabernacle
       </a>
       and later the
       <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">
        Temple in Jerusalem
       </a>
       , the focus of the Passover festival was the Passover sacrifice (Hebrew
       <i>
        korban Pesach
       </i>
       ), also known as the Paschal lamb, eaten during the
       <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">
        Passover Seder
       </a>
       on the 15th of Nisan. Every family large enough to completely consume a young lamb or wild goat was required to offer one for sacrifice at the Jewish Temple on the afternoon of the 14th day of Nisan (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0409.htm#11" rel="nofollow">
        Numbers 9:11
       </a>
       ), and eat it that night, which was the 15th of Nisan (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblica.com/en-us/bible/online-bible/?osis=niv:Exodus.12:6–12:6" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:6
       </a>
       ). If the family was too small to finish eating the entire offering in one sitting, an offering was made for a group of families. The sacrifice could not be offered with anything leavened (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0223.htm#18" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 23:18
       </a>
       ), and had to be roasted, without its head, feet, or inner organs being removed (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#9" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:9
       </a>
       ) and eaten together with unleavened bread (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
         matzo
        </a>
       </i>
       ) and bitter herbs (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Maror" title="Maror">
         maror
        </a>
       </i>
       ). One had to be careful not to break any bones from the offering (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#46" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:46
       </a>
       ), and none of the meat could be left over by morning (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#10" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:10
       </a>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0223.htm#18" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 23:18
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of the Passover sacrifice's status as a sacred offering, the only people allowed to eat it were those who had the obligation to bring the offering. Among those who could not offer or eat the Passover lamb were an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostate" title="Apostate">
        apostate
       </a>
       (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#43" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:43
       </a>
       ), a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" title="Indentured servant">
        servant
       </a>
       (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#45" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:45
       </a>
       ), an
       <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">
        uncircumcised man
       </a>
       (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#48" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:48
       </a>
       ), a person in a state of
       <a href="/wiki/Tumah_and_taharah" title="Tumah and taharah">
        ritual impurity
       </a>
       , except when a majority of Jews are in such a state (
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesahim" title="Pesahim">
         Pesahim
        </a>
       </i>
       66b), and a non-Jew. The offering had to be made before a
       <a href="/wiki/Quorum" title="Quorum">
        quorum
       </a>
       of 30 (
       <i>
        Pesahim
       </i>
       64b). In the Temple, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levites" title="Levites">
        Levites
       </a>
       sang
       <a href="/wiki/Hallel" title="Hallel">
        Hallel
       </a>
       while the
       <a href="/wiki/Kohen" title="Kohen">
        priests
       </a>
       performed the sacrificial service. Men and women were equally obligated regarding the offering (
       <i>
        Pesahim
       </i>
       91b).
      </p>
      <p>
       Women were obligated, as men, to perform the Korban Pesach and to participate in a Seder.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Today">
        Today
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Today, in the absence of the Temple, when no sacrifices are offered or eaten, the
       <a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">
        mitzvah
       </a>
       of the
       <i>
        Korban Pesach
       </i>
       is memorialized in the
       <i>
        Seder Korban Pesach
       </i>
       , a set of scriptural and Rabbinic passages dealing with the Passover sacrifice, customarily recited after the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mincha" title="Mincha">
         Mincha
        </a>
       </i>
       (afternoon prayer) service on the 14th on Nisan,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitov_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kitov-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in the form of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Zeroa" title="Zeroa">
         zeroa
        </a>
       </i>
       , a symbolic food placed on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passover_Seder_Plate" title="Passover Seder Plate">
        Passover Seder Plate
       </a>
       (but not eaten), which is usually a roasted
       <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">
        shankbone
       </a>
       (or a chicken wing or neck). The eating of the
       <a href="/wiki/Afikoman" title="Afikoman">
        afikoman
       </a>
       substitutes for the eating of the
       <i>
        Korban Pesach
       </i>
       at the end of the Seder meal (
       <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">
        Mishnah
       </a>
       Pesachim 119a). Many
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
        Sephardi Jews
       </a>
       have the custom of eating lamb or goat meat during the Seder in memory of the
       <i>
        Korban Pesach
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Removing_all_chametz">
        Removing all chametz
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Chametz#Removal_of_chametz" title="Chametz">
        Chametz § Removal of chametz
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BiurChametz2010.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BiurChametz2010.jpg/250px-BiurChametz2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BiurChametz2010.jpg/375px-BiurChametz2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/BiurChametz2010.jpg/500px-BiurChametz2010.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BiurChametz2010.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Burning chametz on the morning before Passover begins
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Chametz#What_is_chametz?" title="Chametz">
        Chametz
       </a>
       (חמץ, "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leavening" title="Leavening">
        leavening
       </a>
       ") is made from one of five types of grains
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pomerantz_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pomerantz-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       combined with water and left to stand for more than eighteen minutes. The consumption, keeping, and owning of
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       is forbidden during Passover. Yeast and fermentation are not themselves forbidden as seen for example by wine, which is required, rather than merely permitted. According to Halakha, the ownership of such
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       is also proscribed.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Chametz
       </i>
       does not include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">
        baking soda
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">
        baking powder
       </a>
       or like products. Although these are defined in English as leavening agents, they leaven by chemical reaction, not by biological fermentation. Thus, bagels, waffles and pancakes made with baking soda and matzo meal are considered permissible, while bagels made with sourdough and pancakes and waffles made with yeast are prohibited.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Torah commandments regarding
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        To remove all
        <i>
         chametz
        </i>
        from one's home, including things made with chametz, before the first day of Passover (
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#15" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 12:15
        </a>
        ). It may be simply used up, thrown out (historically, destroyed by burning), or given or sold to non-Jews (or non-Samaritans, as the case may be).
       </li>
       <li>
        To refrain from eating
        <i>
         chametz
        </i>
        or mixtures containing
        <i>
         chametz
        </i>
        during Passover (
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0213.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 13:3
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#20" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 12:20
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0516.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
         Deuteronomy 16:3
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Not to possess
        <i>
         chametz
        </i>
        in one's domain (i.e. home, office, car, etc.) during Passover (
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#19" rel="nofollow">
         Exodus 12:19
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0516.htm#4" rel="nofollow">
         Deuteronomy 16:4
        </a>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Observant Jews spend the weeks before Passover in a flurry of thorough housecleaning, to remove every morsel of
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       from every part of the home.
       <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">
        Jewish law
       </a>
       requires the elimination of
       <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">
        olive
       </a>
       -sized or larger quantities of leavening from one's possession, but most housekeeping goes beyond this. Even the cracks of kitchen counters are thoroughly scrubbed, for example, to remove any traces of flour and yeast, however small. Any item or implement that has handled
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       is generally put away and not used during Passover.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Hotel" title="Hotel">
        hotels
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Resort" title="Resort">
        resorts
       </a>
       , and even
       <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">
        cruise ships
       </a>
       across
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        America
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
        Israel
       </a>
       also undergo a thorough housecleaning to make their premises "kosher for Pesach" to cater to observant Jews.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretations_for_abstinence_from_leaven_or_yeast">
        Interpretations for abstinence from leaven or yeast
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some scholars suggest that the command to abstain from leavened food or yeast suggests that sacrifices offered to God involve the offering of objects in "their least altered state", that would be nearest to the way in which they were initially made by God.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bokser_41-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bokser-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenberg_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greenberg-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to other scholars the absence of leaven or yeast means that leaven or yeast symbolizes corruption and spoiling.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bokser_41-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bokser-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarna_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sarna-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally, there is a tradition of not eating matzoh (flat unleavened bread) in the 30 days before Passover begins so that there will be an increased appetite for it during Passover itself.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_chametz">
        Sale of chametz
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Chametz#Mechirah_practices" title="Chametz">
        Chametz § Mechirah practices
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg">
         <img alt="A narrow supermarket aisle, under strip fluorescent lighting, with sections blocked off by white plastic sheeting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2648" data-file-width="3388" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg/220px-Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg/330px-Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg/440px-Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leavened_foods_concealed_behind_plastic_at_Jerusalem_supermarket_during_Passover.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Chametz
         </i>
         foods blocked from purchase during Passover in a Jerusalem supermarket
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Chametz
       </i>
       may be sold rather than discarded, especially in the case of relatively valuable forms such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">
        liquor
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">
        distilled
       </a>
       from wheat, with the products being repurchased afterward. In some cases, they may never leave the house, instead being formally sold while remaining in the original owner's possession in a locked cabinet until they can be repurchased after the holiday. Modern observance may also include sealing cabinets and drawers which contain "Chametz" shut by using adhesive tape, which serves a similar purpose to a lock but also shows evidence of tampering. Although the practice of selling "Chametz" dates back many years, some Reform rabbinical authorities have come to regard it with disdain – since the supposed "new owner" never takes actual possession of the goods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The sale of
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       may also be conducted communally via a
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">
        rabbi
       </a>
       , who becomes the "agent" for all the community's Jews through a halakhic procedure called a
       <i>
        kinyan
       </i>
       (acquisition). Each householder must put aside all the
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       he is selling into a box or cupboard, and the rabbi enters into a contract to sell all the
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       to a non-Jew (who is not obligated to celebrate the commandments) in exchange for a small
       <a href="/wiki/Down_payment" title="Down payment">
        down payment
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        e.g.
       </i>
       $1.00), with the remainder due after Passover. This sale is considered completely binding according to Halakha, and at any time during the holiday, the buyer may come to take or partake of his property. The rabbi then re-purchases the goods for less than they were sold at the end of the holiday.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_leaven">
        Search for leaven
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="38"/>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist">
         <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">
          Wikisource
         </a>
         has original text related to this article:
         <div style="margin-left: 10px;">
          <b>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Translation:Talmud/Seder_Moed/Tractate_Pesachim/2a" title="wikisource:Translation:Talmud/Seder Moed/Tractate Pesachim/2a">
            Talmud's introduction to checking for chametz and defining
            <i>
             or
            </i>
            (literally, "light") (Tractate Pesachim 2a)
           </a>
          </b>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       On the night of the fourteenth of
       <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">
        Nisan
       </a>
       , the night before the
       <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">
        Passover Seder
       </a>
       (after nightfall on the evening before Passover eve), Jews do a formal search in their homes known as
       <i>
        bedikat chametz
       </i>
       for any possible remaining leaven (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chametz" title="Chametz">
         chametz
        </a>
       </i>
       ). The
       <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
        Talmudic
       </a>
       sages instructed that a search for
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       be made in every home, place of work, or any place where
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       may have been brought during the year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When the first Seder is on a Saturday night, the search is conducted on the preceding Thursday night (thirteenth of Nisan) as
       <a href="/wiki/Eve_of_Passover_on_Shabbat" title="Eve of Passover on Shabbat">
        <i>
         chametz
        </i>
        cannot be burned during Shabbat
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
        Talmud
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesahim" title="Pesahim">
        Pesahim
       </a>
       (p. 2a) derives from the
       <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
        Torah
       </a>
       that the search for
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       be conducted by the light of a candle and therefore is done at night, and although the final destruction of the
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       (usually by burning it in a small bonfire) is done on the next morning, the blessing is made at night because the search is both in preparation for and part of the commandments to remove and destroy all
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       from one's possession.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Blessing_for_search_of_chametz_and_nullification_of_chametz">
        Blessing for search of chametz and nullification of chametz
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Before the search is begun there is a special
       <a href="/wiki/Berakhah" title="Berakhah">
        blessing
       </a>
       . If several people or family members assist in the search then only one person, usually the head of that family recites the blessing having in mind to include everyone present:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor_50-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Blessed are You, Hashem our God, King of the universe, Who has sanctified us with his commandments and has commanded us concerning the removal of chametz.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In Hebrew:
      </p>
      <p>
       ברוך אתה יהוה אלהינו מלך העולם אשר קדשנו במצותיו וצונו על בעור חמץ
      </p>
      <p>
       The search is then usually conducted by the head of the household joined by his family including children under the supervision of their parents.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is customary to turn off the lights and conduct the search by
       <a href="/wiki/Candle" title="Candle">
        candlelight
       </a>
       , using a feather and a wooden spoon: candlelight effectively illuminates corners without casting shadows; the feather can dust crumbs out of their hiding places; and the wooden spoon which collects the crumbs can be burned the next day with the
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       . However, most contemporary Jewish-Orthodox authorities permit using a flashlight, while some strongly encourage it due to the danger coupled with using a candle.
      </p>
      <p>
       Because the house is assumed to have been thoroughly cleaned by the night before Passover, there is some concern that making a blessing over the search for
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       will be in vain (
       <i>
        bracha l'vatala
       </i>
       ) if nothing is found. Thus, 10 morsels of bread or cereal smaller than the size of an olive are traditionally hidden throughout the house in order to ensure that some
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       will be found.
      </p>
      <p>
       Upon conclusion of the search, with all the small pieces safely wrapped up and put in one bag or place, to be burned the next morning, the following is said:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Any chametz or leaven that is in my possession which I have not seen and have not removed and do not know about should be annulled and become ownerless like the dust of the earth.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Original declaration as recited in
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor_50-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       כל חמירא וחמיעא דאכא ברשותי דלא חמתה ודלא בערתה ודלא ידענא לה לבטל ולהוי הפקר כעפרא דארעא
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Morning_of_14th_of_Nissan">
        Morning of 14th of Nissan
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Note that if the 14th of Nissan is
       <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">
        Shabbat
       </a>
       , many of the below will be celebrated on the 13th instead
       <a href="/wiki/Eve_of_Passover_on_Shabbat" title="Eve of Passover on Shabbat">
        due to restrictions in place during Shabbat
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fast_of_the_Firstborn">
        Fast of the Firstborn
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Fast_of_the_Firstborn" title="Fast of the Firstborn">
        Fast of the Firstborn
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Siyum" title="Siyum">
        siyum
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       On the day preceding the first Passover seder (or on Thursday morning preceding the seder, when the first seder falls on
       <a href="/wiki/Motza%27ei_Shabbat" title="Motza'ei Shabbat">
        Motza'ei Shabbat
       </a>
       ), firstborn sons are commanded to celebrate the
       <a href="/wiki/Fast_of_the_Firstborn" title="Fast of the Firstborn">
        Fast of the Firstborn
       </a>
       which commemorates the salvation of the Hebrew firstborns. According to
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#29" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:29
       </a>
       , God struck down all Egyptian firstborns while the Israelites were not affected. However, it is customary for synagogues to conduct a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Siyum" title="Siyum">
         siyum
        </a>
       </i>
       (ceremony marking the completion of a section of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torah_learning" title="Torah learning">
        Torah learning
       </a>
       ) right after
       <a href="/wiki/Shacharit" title="Shacharit">
        morning prayers
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Seudat_mitzvah" title="Seudat mitzvah">
        celebratory meal
       </a>
       that follows cancels the firstborn's obligation to fast.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Burning_and_nullification_of_chametz">
        Burning and nullification of chametz
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       On the morning of the 14th of
       <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">
        Nisan
       </a>
       , any leavened products that remain in the householder's possession, along with the 10 morsels of bread from the previous night's search, are burned (
       <i>
        s'rayfat chametz
       </i>
       ). The head of the household repeats the declaration of
       <i>
        biyur chametz
       </i>
       , declaring any
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       that may not have been found to be null and void "as the dust of the earth":
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Any chametz or leaven that is in my possession which I have not seen and have not removed and do not know about should be annulled and become ownerless like the dust of the earth.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Original declaration as recited in
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
        Aramaic
       </a>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor_50-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-ArtScroll_Pesach_Machzor-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       כל חמירא וחמיעא דאכא ברשותי דלא חמתה ודלא בערתה ודלא ידענא לה לבטל ולהוי הפקר כעפרא דארעא
      </p>
      <p>
       Should more
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       actually be found in the house during the Passover holiday, it must be burnt as soon as possible.
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       , which can be eaten any day of the year except during Passover, kosher for Passover foods can be eaten year-round. They need not be burnt or otherwise discarded after the holiday ends.
      </p>
      <p>
       The historic "Paschal lamb"
       <a href="/wiki/Passover_sacrifice" title="Passover sacrifice">
        Passover sacrifice
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Korban Pesach
       </i>
       ) has not been brought following the Romans' destruction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">
        Second Jewish temple
       </a>
       approximately two thousand years ago, and it is therefore still not part of the modern Jewish holiday.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the Paschal lamb is still a principal feature of Falashah, Karaite and Samaritan observance.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the times when the Jewish Temples stood, the lamb was slaughtered and cooked on the evening of Passover and was completely consumed before the morning as described in
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#3" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:3–11
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Separate_kosher_for_Passover_utensils_and_dishes">
        Separate kosher for Passover utensils and dishes
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Due to the Torah injunction not to eat
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       during Passover (
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0212.htm#15" rel="nofollow">
        Exodus 12:15
       </a>
       ), observant families typically own complete sets of serving dishes, glassware and silverware (and in some cases, even separate dishwashers and sinks) which have never come into contact with
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       , for use only during Passover. Under certain circumstances, some
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       utensils can be immersed in boiling water (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hagalah" title="Hagalah">
         hagalat keilim
        </a>
       </i>
       ) to purge them of any traces of
       <i>
        chametz
       </i>
       that may have accumulated during the year. Many
       <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
        Sephardic
       </a>
       families thoroughly wash their year-round glassware and then use it for Passover, as the Sephardic position is that
       <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">
        glass
       </a>
       does not absorb enough traces of food to present a problem. Similarly, ovens may be used for Passover either by setting the self-cleaning function to the highest degree for a certain period of time, or by applying a
       <a href="/wiki/Blow_torch" title="Blow torch">
        blow torch
       </a>
       to the interior until the oven glows red hot (a process called
       <i>
        libun gamur
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJblowtorch_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WSJblowtorch-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Matzah">
        Matzah
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
        Matzo
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1136" data-file-width="1755" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg/250px-Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg/375px-Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg/500px-Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Machine-made_Shmura_Matzo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Machine made
         <i>
          shmura matza
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A symbol of the Passover holiday is
       <a href="/wiki/Matzo" title="Matzo">
        matzo
       </a>
       , an unleavened flatbread made solely from flour and water which is continually worked from mixing through baking, so that it is not allowed to rise. Matzo may be made by machine or by hand. The Torah contains an instruction to eat matzo, specifically, on the first night of Passover and to eat only unleavened bread (in practice, matzo) during the entire week of Passover.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Consequently, the eating of matzo figures prominently in the
       <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">
        Passover Seder
       </a>
       . There are several explanations for this.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Torah says that it is because the Hebrews left Egypt with such haste that there was no time to allow baked bread to rise; thus flat, unleavened bread, matzo, is a reminder of the rapid departure of the Exodus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other scholars teach that in the time of the Exodus, matzo was commonly baked for the purpose of traveling because it preserved well and was light to carry (making it similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Hardtack" title="Hardtack">
        hardtack
       </a>
       ), suggesting that matzo was baked intentionally for the long journey ahead.
      </p>
      <p>
       Matzo has also been called
       <i>
        Lechem Oni
       </i>
       (Hebrew: "bread of poverty"). There is an attendant explanation that matzo serves as a symbol to remind Jews what it is like to be a poor slave and to promote humility, appreciate freedom, and avoid the inflated ego symbolized by more luxurious leavened bread.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shmura_Matzo2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Shmura_Matzo2.jpg/250px-Shmura_Matzo2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Shmura_Matzo2.jpg/375px-Shmura_Matzo2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Shmura_Matzo2.jpg/500px-Shmura_Matzo2.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
